  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_f7e0e6cc-c25d-4c12-a2e7-445e3b665c34'
      });
    }

<div id="descriptionContainer">
  
      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer1513106786170324211">The concluding volume of the Border trilogy. In this magnificent new novel, the National Book Award-winning author of <i>All the Pretty Horses</i> and <i>The Crossing</i> fashions a darkly beautiful elegy for the American frontier. It is 1952 and John Grady Cole and Billy Parham are working as ranch hands in New Mexico, not far from the proving grounds of Alamogordo and the cities of El</span>
  <span id="freeText1513106786170324211" style="display:none">The concluding volume of the Border trilogy. In this magnificent new novel, the National Book Award-winning author of <i>All the Pretty Horses</i> and <i>The Crossing</i> fashions a darkly beautiful elegy for the American frontier. It is 1952 and John Grady Cole and Billy Parham are working as ranch hands in New Mexico, not far from the proving grounds of Alamogordo and the cities of El Paso and Juarez. Their life is made up of trail drives and horse auctions and stories told by campfire light. They value that life all the more because they know it is about to change forever.<br /><br />The change comes when John Grady falls in love with a beautiful, ill-starred Mexican prostitute and sets in motion a chain of events as violent as they are unstoppable. Haunting in its beauty, filled with sorrow, humor, and awe, <i>Cities of the Plain</i> is a genuine American epic.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1513106786170324211" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer101738751" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18056302168407801174"><b>I met Cormac McCarthy and he transcribed our conversation</b> about <i>Cities of the Plain</i>:<br/><br/>The author asked, Whad'ya think about the book? <br/>The last in the trilogy?<br/>That's it. <br/>It was alright, Jason said.<br/>What was alright?<br/><i>Cities of the Plain</i><br/>What specifically?<br/>The simple language and the economy of words and the lack of punctuation, quotations especially. How you made simple things like chores seem interesting and wonderful.<br/>That's fair. It's actually harder to write like that than you think.<br/>I bet.<br/>Was it bett</span>
  <span id="freeText18056302168407801174" style="display:none"><b>I met Cormac McCarthy and he transcribed our conversation</b> about <i>Cities of the Plain</i>:<br /><br />The author asked, Whad'ya think about the book? <br />The last in the trilogy?<br />That's it. <br />It was alright, Jason said.<br />What was alright?<br /><i>Cities of the Plain</i><br />What specifically?<br />The simple language and the economy of words and the lack of punctuation, quotations especially. How you made simple things like chores seem interesting and wonderful.<br />That's fair. It's actually harder to write like that than you think.<br />I bet.<br />Was it better than the first two, the first two books I mean?<br />No.<br />Why?<br />I thought the second book was better.<br />The author shifted in his seat and lit a cigarette and picked musingly at a fingernail with a jag in it. He looked from the cargazón de espaldas of his house and toward the wall of scrub that marked the edge of some New Mexico wilderness. Do you like my polysyndeton? <br />Polysynda what?<br />Polysyndeton. It's where I use a lot of correlative conjunctions to string out sentences instead of using commas.<br />Oh. I reckon.<br />Only 3 stars. What could I have done better?<br />Don't figure I'm the best person to ask about that.<br />You count. I write for people like you.<br />Still.<br />No, lemme hear.<br />Then, I guess you could of jazzed up some of the action especially toward the first half of the book. <br />The story didn't draw you in?<br />No sir it didn't.<br />There was a theme I was huntin' for, that first half. I wanted life to seem timeless and I did that through the sustained description of routine life for several vaqueros.<br />I understand.<br />The author exhaled through both nostrils making an opaque column of smoke that stretched uniformly to the wooden cubierta. Were the characters likable?<br />I liked Billy and John Grady and Mac. I liked the part when they saved them puppies in the traprock escarpment.<br />That's a critical part. Those boys had to kill the adult dogs in order to save the pups. It was an exchange of life. Those pups woulda likely died out there for want of food.<br />Yep. They kept having to kill calves to feed the pups. Once them calves got bigger, the dogs would've been outta food.<br />That's exactly right.<br />I liked the knife fight too and how John Grady was fallin' so in love with that whore. <br />Good.<br />She was very young.<br />That's right.<br />And I like when you mash up two words.<br />You mean when I make one word out of an adjective and a noun?<br />Yes.<br />I do that quite often.<br />You do it on almost every page.<br />About.<br />Hey, I understand your writing. It's just, I gave 3 stars because your second book had 4 stars and since I didn't think your third book was better than the second, I couldn't give the same rating.<br />Okay.<br />But I did really like the descriptions you made of the environment and the way the sky looked and how a man would have felt looking out across the llanos. And I even liked how you dropped a lot of spanish words in the book, almost as if you was searching for the right word and the absolute right word wasn't an english word but a spanish word. And then you used some big words that I had to look up.<br />Uh-huh. I did that. He flipped the cigarette in a flection out into the dirt. Is there anything I wrote that you didn't like?<br />The short dialogue. <br />How's that?<br />The dialogue was always so staccato.<br />That's how they talk. It's realistic.<br />Yes sir.<br />But you said you liked my economy of words, earlier you said that.<br />I know what I said.<br />Well. That's how I wrote my dialogue.<br />I reckon you did.<br />Well, then, what about the dialogue you didn't like?<br />Maybe it was the lack of quotations. Made it hard to read. I don't know.<br />That's fair. I done that in most of my books. <br />You know what Mr. McCarthy? I especially liked the very last part after Billy was grown up and met that vagabundo and he went into that bizarre tirade about the dream he had and what it meant to him and therefore what it meant for all of mankind.<br />I only did that once in this book.<br />I know.<br />You liked that huh? You think I should have done that more?<br />Yes sir I do.<br />Hmmm.<br />When you do that, when you make your characters get all fantastic, those are some good parts.<br />I try to divine the essence of the human condition, Jason.<br />Right.<br />And you liked that?<br />I liked it very much.<br />But once wasn't enough?<br />No. The second book was better.<br />Because it had more episodes where my characters had fantastic tirades?<br />That's right.<br />Mr. McCarthy crossed his arms and put his boots on the barandilla and tipped his chair back on two legs. He looked at the skyline just above the scrub in the distance. The world had a light gauzy dome of high cloud. The sun was getting low in that direction, but the color of the sky was as if it was still sizzlin', a couple of sun dogs on either side. The author asked, Did you like the whore?<br />She was young.<br />Yes.<br />You made her sound pretty.<br />Yes.<br />I figure I wouldn't want to marry a whore.<br />The others tried to stop him.<br />But they didn't.<br />No.<br />I don't think I would have died for her.<br />You aint John Grady.<br />No sir.<br />Would you recommend <i>Cities of the Plains</i> to your friends? He scratched his ankle deep down the inside of his boot.<br />I would.<br />Do you think the books can be read individually or should be read as a trilogy?<br />Well, I can only answer for myself.<br />I aint askin' anybody but you.<br />What's the question?<br />Can they be read separately or should they be read as a whole?<br />As a whole. Altogether, I reckon.<br />Do you think I should write a fourth book?<br />Ever'body's old and dead now.<br />Kind of a prequel.<br />Kind of a prequel?<br />Uh-huh.<br />No.<br />You don't think?<br />No, it's just right now, especially that second book.<br />Jason slapped the dust off his trouser thighs and stood for awhile lookin' out toward the sun. He took a final sip from the glass of ice lemonade and set it back on the paso among all the other water rings that sweated off the glass. Mr. McCarthy, he said.<br />Cormac.<br />Mr. McCarthy, sir, it's been a pleasure.<br />Pleasure's mine.<br />Alright, but it's been nice talkin' to you and learnin' what you put into them books.<br />I appreciate the feedback.<br />From me?<br />Yes, you read all 3 books, makes you as close an expert as me.<br />Uhh, I don't reckon I understand what you just said.<br />Look, Jason, a writer spends an awful lot of time putting words on paper and figurin' and refigurin' how to change those words so it has an effect on the reader, someone like you.<br />I understand.<br />So if my writing doesn't have an effect, well, then...<br />Then it don't mean nothing.<br />No, it means something. But then it means something only to me.<br />I see.<br />Do you?<br />Sure.<br />If my writing doesn't affect you, then my writing is nothing more than a glorified journal entry. If it don't sell, then it stays with me.<br />So you mean to share it with folks like me.<br />Correct.<br />Yes sir.<br />What's that face your making?<br />I still don't like the idea of a prequel.<br />Don't worry 'bout that.<br />You're not going to write one.<br />No.<br />Good.<br />That story's over.<br />That's how I feel about it.<br />The author rose and took Jason's hand in his and shook it and shook it again and when they let go there was an understanding among men that cascaded through all the understandings between men and had arrived at this point firmly, and hung there, deep, like a great granite batholith. Take care reader.<br />I will.<br />Bye.<br />Oh, one last thing.<br />Anything.<br />When you transcribe this discussion, would you send me a copy.<br />For what?<br />So I can put it on this computer Goodreads thing.<br />I can do that.<br />Much obliged.<br />Take care then.<br />Bye Mr. McCarthy.<br /> <br />New words: dishabille, peened, niello, fard, replevin, ned, maguey, quirted, <br /> <br /><br />Cool sentences:<br /> <br /><i>There were grounds in the bottom of the cup and he swirled the cup and looked at them. Then he swirled them the other way as if he'd put them back the way they'd been.</i>(p. 138)<br /> <br /><i>Billy flipped the cigarette out across the yard. It was already dark enough that it made an arc in the fading light. Arcs within the arc.</i>(p. 147)<br /> <br /><i>When they reached the trail along the western edge of the floodplain the sun was up behind the mesa and the light that overshot the plain crossed to the rocks above them so that they rode out the remnant of the night in a deep blue sink with the new day falling slowly down about them.</i>(p. 171)<br /> <br /><i>The ceiling of the room was of concrete and bore the impression of the boards used to form it, the concrete knots and nailheads and the fossil arc of the circlesaw's blade from some mountain sawmill. There was a single sooty bulb that burned there with a grudging orange light and a millermoth that patrolled it in random clockwise orbits.</i>(p. 208)<br /> <br />The word polysydeton was given to my by Isaiah H.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="18056302168407801174" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_2077192410" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2077192410" />
    <a title="Jaline" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7234171-jaline"><img alt="Jaline" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1530498058p2/7234171.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2077192410?book_show_action=true">Oct 11, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jaline" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jaline" href="/user/show/7234171-jaline">Jaline</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7234171-jaline?shelf=xx2018-completed">xx2018-completed</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2077192410" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5338925802969403862"><b>The Border Trilogy – Part 3 of 3</b><br/><br/>In this final novel of The Border Trilogy, both John Grady Cole and Billy Parham are working at Mac Ranch, owned by a fellow named McGovern. Everyone calls him Mac, and all the cowboys on the ranch know that their time together appears to be limited as the government plans to take over huge tracts of land in the area, including Mac Ranch.<br/><br/>John Grady is now 19 and Billy is 28. They have become good friends through sharing their stories of Mexico and working together</span>
  <span id="freeText5338925802969403862" style="display:none"><b>The Border Trilogy – Part 3 of 3</b><br /><br />In this final novel of The Border Trilogy, both John Grady Cole and Billy Parham are working at Mac Ranch, owned by a fellow named McGovern. Everyone calls him Mac, and all the cowboys on the ranch know that their time together appears to be limited as the government plans to take over huge tracts of land in the area, including Mac Ranch.<br /><br />John Grady is now 19 and Billy is 28. They have become good friends through sharing their stories of Mexico and working together every day. They have one major area of disagreement. John Grady is once again madly in love – this time with a young Mexican prostitute just a short (and very expensive) trip across the border. She is in love with him, too, but there are definite problems in the relationship. Not least is that Magdalena (her real name) works for a pimp who also happens to be in love with her.<br /><br />This novel has many incidents that occur on the ranch, and also in the Mexican town. Once again, Cormac McCarthy grabbed my attention with his writing: it is bold, beautifully descriptive, sensorially alive, and with dialogue that sounds real. I am in awe of his ability to write believably of situations where the cruelties of nature and of man collide, separate, and then clash again.<br /><br />There is an epilogue at the end of this novel that I suspect some readers may not enjoy. It takes a giant leap, carrying the reader along, 50 years into the future. Billy had long returned to his nomad ways and he isn’t any richer financially than when he’d started out as a boy. He is unconcerned as his life has always been lived that way. One night he meets a man that he shares some crackers with, sitting on the concrete footing of an underpass.<br /><br />This section is almost like a short story. Although I already knew a great deal about Billy’s younger days, the 50 year gap time contained a lot of hard living, too. However, “the narrator” Billy is sharing this spot with has a great deal to say, and the two of them sit discussing life, dreams, and what amounts to philosophy. Some of it is down-home and some of it sophisticated. I found it fascinating, even though the pace is wound down substantially from the main chapters of this book. As always with Cormac McCarthy’s novels, there is much more substance than what meets the eye if the reader is willing to follow the pace Mr. McCarthy sets.<br /><br />As with the other two books in this Trilogy, I enjoyed this novel immensely and I would recommend this Trilogy to people who enjoy well-crafted stories, and authentic characters. The writing is so good it often doesn’t offer its gifts until the mind takes over after setting the book down and deeper processes and meanings come through.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="5338925802969403862" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer42423900471355361">For me the least successful of the trilogy though there was still much to love. This brings back the central characters of books one and two. It's essentially a love story. John Grady Cole falls in love with a young girl who suffers from epilepsy and works in a Mexican brothel. His aim to rescue and marry her. The problem is her pimp is very possessive of her. The most moving relationships though are those the boy shares with the elderly Mac and his friend Billy. This novel is less violent than </span>
  <span id="freeText42423900471355361" style="display:none">For me the least successful of the trilogy though there was still much to love. This brings back the central characters of books one and two. It's essentially a love story. John Grady Cole falls in love with a young girl who suffers from epilepsy and works in a Mexican brothel. His aim to rescue and marry her. The problem is her pimp is very possessive of her. The most moving relationships though are those the boy shares with the elderly Mac and his friend Billy. This novel is less violent than the others I've read by Macarthy but follows the usual formula. Lots of fabulous poetic writing, brilliant descriptions of the natural world and the usual cast of seers who provide a marvellous philosophical structure.</span>

<div id="review_103453203" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/103453203" />
    <a title="Matt" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1630419-matt"><img alt="Matt" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1225153139p2/1630419.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/103453203?book_show_action=true">May 19, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Matt" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Matt" href="/user/show/1630419-matt">Matt</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1630419-matt?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1630419-matt?shelf=west">west</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer103453203" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5944880168460344814">The Border Trilogy finale, the ending--at least *an* ending.<br/><br/>I greatly enjoyed <a href='http://www.amazon.com/gp/product/0679747192?ie=UTF8&tag=mooncowboycom-20&linkCode=as2&camp=1789&creative=390957&creativeASIN=0679747192' rel='nofollow noopener'>Cities of the Plain</a><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380335096i/694167.gif' width='1' height='1' alt='' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/>. The book was much more dialogue-driven than the previous two--moreso than most McCarthy. It read quite like a screenplay (honestly I'm surprised there's no adaptation in the works--no Matt Damon please). Landscape descriptions, landscape as a character itself, is toned down, replaced with scene and scenario, the near-exciting humdrum of cowboy ranching life, a moribund profession and way of life. B</span>
  <span id="freeText5944880168460344814" style="display:none">The Border Trilogy finale, the ending--at least *an* ending.<br><br>I greatly enjoyed <a href="http://www.amazon.com/gp/product/0679747192?ie=UTF8&amp;tag=mooncowboycom-20&amp;linkCode=as2&amp;camp=1789&amp;creative=390957&amp;creativeASIN=0679747192" rel="nofollow noopener">Cities of the Plain</a><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380335096i/694167.gif" width="1" height="1" alt="" class="gr-hostedUserImg">. The book was much more dialogue-driven than the previous two--moreso than most McCarthy. It read quite like a screenplay (honestly I'm surprised there's no adaptation in the works--no Matt Damon please). Landscape descriptions, landscape as a character itself, is toned down, replaced with scene and scenario, the near-exciting humdrum of cowboy ranching life, a moribund profession and way of life. Billy Parham has seemingly matured past his conflicted downtroddenness, his inability to get or keep what he wants from <a href="http://www.amazon.com/gp/product/0679760849?ie=UTF8&amp;tag=mooncowboycom-20&amp;linkCode=as2&amp;camp=1789&amp;creative=390957&amp;creativeASIN=0679760849" rel="nofollow noopener">The Crossing</a><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380335096i/694168.gif" width="1" height="1" alt="" class="gr-hostedUserImg">. He's John Grady's brother, father figure, his confidant. They are each other's brother, with John Grady filling in for Boyd, bringing out Billy's protectiveness. Billy has a voice of reason, a pragmatic and fatalistic outlook. John Grady is ever the romantic, pursuing his desire with unfailing optimism and hope. Billy's intentions of holding him back are frivolous. The two are quite different, yet see in each other something of value, and it's their brotherly chemistry, conversation and care for one another that sucks the reader in, capturing their emotions entirely.<br><br>The story is slow to begin, but once it picks up it doesn't stop. Though McCarthy's books always leave me reeling, this one carried much emotional weight in both of its "endings." I still run some of Billy's last words through my head, and think of the power this story holds.<br><br>Highly recommended, though if you're going to read this it all, read the first two first. (For reference, my favorite of the trilogy was The Crossing, followed by Cities of the Plain, then <a href="http://www.amazon.com/gp/product/0679744398?ie=UTF8&amp;tag=mooncowboycom-20&amp;linkCode=as2&amp;camp=1789&amp;creative=390957&amp;creativeASIN=0679744398" rel="nofollow noopener">All the Pretty Horses</a><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380335096i/694169.gif" width="1" height="1" alt="" class="gr-hostedUserImg">--though each book is a masterpiece in itself.)<br><br>--- ---<br>Some select quotes, ordered they themselves tell a story:<br>--- ---<br><br>"A man is always right to pursue the thing he loves." (199)<br><br>"a thing once set in motion has no ending in this world until the last witness has passed" (205)<br><br>"there are no crossroads. Our decisions do not have some alternative. We may contemplate a choice but we pursue one path only." (286)<br><br>"... when things are gone they're gone. They aint comin back." (126)<br><br>"every act which has no heart will be found out in the end" (196)<br><br>"The world past, the world to come. ... Above all a knowing deep in the bone that beauty and loss are one." (126)</span>
  <a data-text-id="5944880168460344814" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_181679618" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/181679618" />
    <a title="Daniel Villines" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2266055-daniel-villines"><img alt="Daniel Villines" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1536179787p2/2266055.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/181679618?book_show_action=true">Jul 03, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Daniel Villines" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Daniel Villines" href="/user/show/2266055-daniel-villines">Daniel Villines</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer181679618" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18191733537957951926">Second Reading: January 2017<br/><br/>If <i>All the Pretty Horses</i> is a story about life happening without any way of stopping it, then <i>Cities of the Plain</i> is its perfect counterweight. This is a beautiful raw-earth story about forging ahead with one's dreams in spite of challenges and difficulties. And only a character such as John Grady Cole, who had to accept so much of life in his first appearance in <i>All the Pretty Horses</i>, could have the courage and kindness necessary to push through life in this second a</span>
  <span id="freeText18191733537957951926" style="display:none">Second Reading: January 2017<br /><br />If <i>All the Pretty Horses</i> is a story about life happening without any way of stopping it, then <i>Cities of the Plain</i> is its perfect counterweight. This is a beautiful raw-earth story about forging ahead with one's dreams in spite of challenges and difficulties. And only a character such as John Grady Cole, who had to accept so much of life in his first appearance in <i>All the Pretty Horses</i>, could have the courage and kindness necessary to push through life in this second appearance to make his dreams come true.<br /><br />---<br /><br />First Read January 2015<br /><br />McCarthy captures something magical in <i>Cities of the Plain</i>. He captures a fleeting moment in time in the American West right before the slowly creeping forces of the industrial revolution finally found their way into this vast but remote landscape. Right before the time when the traditional roles of cowboys and their horses became obsolete. It is this theme, <i>all good things...</i>, that McCarthy writes about in <i>Cities of the Plain</i>, in so many words, in direct and indirect ways.<br /><br />It's a beautiful story that McCarthy writes, filled with the pure beauty of places that now only partially exist in our modernized world. There are wide fields of grass and sage, distant mountains, and cattle not in pens but grazing on the land until they need to be rounded up. There are beautiful sunsets and moonless skies filled with so many stars that they too shed a faint light upon the ground. It's not that these things do not presently exist. They indeed happen now if you can find the right place, but they certainly do not happen with the innocent knowledge that they are an endless commodity of life.<br /><br />McCarthy also writes about honesty that is so true it can kill, and he writes about love. <br /><br />In addition to his beautiful story, there is McCarthy's style of writing. It's not enough for McCarthy to simply let his readers read his tale. He wants them engaged and thinking about what he has to say. He starts strings of dialogue by only giving the name of the first speaker, all the while eliminating the use of quotation marks. He initiates scenes in which the identification of the main character is only provided through their characteristics. The result of this style of writing is the feeling that I had imagined this story as opposed to ever having read it.</span>
  <a data-text-id="18191733537957951926" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3272960631" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3272960631" />
    <a title="Jessaka" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4659464-jessaka"><img alt="Jessaka" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1485709659p2/4659464.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3272960631?book_show_action=true">Apr 10, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jessaka" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jessaka" href="/user/show/4659464-jessaka">Jessaka</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3272960631" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18018959556787680476">A Real Cowboy Never Sells His Horse<br/><br/>Billy, from “The Crossing,’ is older now and is working on a ranch in Southeastern New Mexico. The year is 1952. John Grady, a character from maybe “All the Pretty Horses,” is there too. <br/><br/>Ranch work can be interesting, that is, if you only talk about the interesting aspects of it. The stories I heard in Creston, CA, a very small cow town, were always interesting,. If they weren’t, they would not have been told. <br/><br/>In this McCarthy book, interesting doesn’t matter, </span>
  <span id="freeText18018959556787680476" style="display:none">A Real Cowboy Never Sells His Horse<br /><br />Billy, from “The Crossing,’ is older now and is working on a ranch in Southeastern New Mexico. The year is 1952. John Grady, a character from maybe “All the Pretty Horses,” is there too. <br /><br />Ranch work can be interesting, that is, if you only talk about the interesting aspects of it. The stories I heard in Creston, CA, a very small cow town, were always interesting,. If they weren’t, they would not have been told. <br /><br />In this McCarthy book, interesting doesn’t matter, even though it mattered greatly to me. Still, I learned a lot: When Billy and some of the guys were driving down the road late one afternoon, they saw a truck pulled over on the side of the road. Mexican men were trying to change a tire, and they didn’t know how or didn’t have the right tools. What? That doesn’t seem plausible. Mexicans can fix anything. Billy stopes and helps them change the tire, and in the process, the details of this as given by McCarthy, has taught me how to change a tire. I knew how anyway. On the way home, an owl hit the windshield, and the following day I learned how to change the windshield of an old pickup truck. But mostly, I learned how boring it can be to be a ranch hand, at least through McCarthy’s eyes. <br /><br />The story picks up after 4 hours of it being narrated to me. And when it does, it is because some cattle were being killed, not by wolves, but by dogs that have become feral. Well, that was short lived as far as excitement goes. <br /><br />The other excitement is that John Grady Cole is in love with a 16-year old prostitute over in Juarez, Mexico. But so is her pimp. John wants to marry her, and I am thinking that this is where she or he gets killed, if not, perhaps, if he gets her to the U.S., everyone on the ranch will be in danger. And what does he really know about her? He continually talks about her beauty, and I think that is why he is so smitten. When John Grady decides to fix up a house for her on the ranch, I begin to feel bad for him. Will she ever get to see it? Would she even want to live alone in that house that is hard to find on the ranch? I even thought about how he sold his gun just so he could sleep with her, and I think that he may need it. One day he asked for an advance on his next month’s wages, $100 That bought a little more time with her. Next, he sells his horse. I always thought that a cowboy and his horse couldn't be parted. <br /><br />So, the story comes to a head when it is time to get the girl, and I was just glad when it was over. I would have given this book 2 stars, but since the prose was good, it gest a 3, even though I didn’t notice his prose since I was so bored.</span>
  <a data-text-id="18018959556787680476" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2680431726?book_show_action=true">Feb 09, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Raul Bimenyimana" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Raul Bimenyimana" href="/user/show/43664238-raul-bimenyimana">Raul Bimenyimana</a>

        rated it

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2680431726" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1991711528101205479">A definite star rating for this would be 3.5. This is because there are parts of this book I loved and others that I didn't. <br/><br/>This is the story of John Grady and his friend Billy. Two cowboys working in country Texas at the borderarea with Mexico. As the title of this book alludes to the biblical story of Sodom and Gomorrah, and Juarez, is the Mexican town where American cowboys go for drink and "excitement". It is during one of such trips that John Grady falls in love with a girl human trafficke</span>
  <span id="freeText1991711528101205479" style="display:none">A definite star rating for this would be 3.5. This is because there are parts of this book I loved and others that I didn't. <br /><br />This is the story of John Grady and his friend Billy. Two cowboys working in country Texas at the borderarea with Mexico. As the title of this book alludes to the biblical story of Sodom and Gomorrah, and Juarez, is the Mexican town where American cowboys go for drink and "excitement". It is during one of such trips that John Grady falls in love with a girl human trafficked into sex work and devices a way to rescue her. <br /><br />A lot of the reviews that were on the front pages and the backcover of this book had the words "brutal", "beauty", "sadness" to describe this work. And it is true all these are found in it. A curious one talked about "masculine romanticism" and I don't quite understand what it means. Perhaps it is because he tells of a love story with cowboys and pimps and violence and death?<br /><br />What I admired most about this book and the other McCarthy books I've read, is how with such few words he paints very rich and detailed scenes.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1991711528101205479" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_144693477" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/144693477" />
    <a title="Chloe" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/70078-chloe"><img alt="Chloe" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1580564408p2/70078.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/144693477?book_show_action=true">Jan 30, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Chloe" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Chloe" href="/user/show/70078-chloe">Chloe</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780330390163" href="/book/show/850066.Cities_of_the_Plain">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/70078-chloe?shelf=open_range">open_range</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/70078-chloe?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer144693477" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10140268930786725553">This has been one hell of a winter of McCarthy for me. Starting in early January I began his award-winning Border Trilogy with much trepidation. Having previously only read his Pulitzer-winning father-son dystopian nightmare, <i>The Road</i>, and found it severely lacking, I was curious to see if McCarthy's previous works were worthy of the acclaim in which they are held. After three weeks of being immersed in one of the most bleak interpretations of humanity and exposure to tragedy that would make eve</span>
  <span id="freeText10140268930786725553" style="display:none">This has been one hell of a winter of McCarthy for me. Starting in early January I began his award-winning Border Trilogy with much trepidation. Having previously only read his Pulitzer-winning father-son dystopian nightmare, <i>The Road</i>, and found it severely lacking, I was curious to see if McCarthy's previous works were worthy of the acclaim in which they are held. After three weeks of being immersed in one of the most bleak interpretations of humanity and exposure to tragedy that would make even the ancient Greeks wince in sympathy, I can easily attest to McCarthy's merits as a thinker and a writer. That said, while still an eminently enjoyable read that I could not make myself put down (even under direct threat of bodily harm), <i>Cities of the Plain</i> is still the weakest of the Border Trilogy. <br /><br />On face it sounds like a mishmash designed to cash in on the name value of two of McCarthy's most haunting characters. John Grady Cole, the lovelorn horse whisperer of <i>All The Pretty Horses</i>, and Billy Parham, the haunted wanderer of <i>The Crossing</i>, are working on a ranch in New Mexico in the early days of 1952. Threatened by plummeting profits and the loss of their grazing land through an eminent domain seizure by a Cold War military looking for the most unwanted, hard-scrabble land on which to test their weapons in the first days of the nuclear arms race. Cowboys in an atomic age, the protagonists know their world is ending and deal with it in their tried and tested ways. Grady Cole by throwing himself into a(nother) forbidden romance, this time no estancia owner's daughter but an epileptic prostitute across the border in Juarez, while Billy Parham rides the range from one end to another trying to outrun the ghosts of his past.<br /><br />I have to admit that for the first two hundred pages I didn't quite understand the point of even including Parham's character as, up to that point, the story focused almost entirely on Grady Cole's fantasy of saving the hooker with the heart of gold. Of course, this being McCarthy, nothing works out as it should and eventually Parham's involvement makes sense as his coterie of shades swells in number and he attempts once more to find justice in Mexico, the country that has peeled away one attachment after another from him. <br /><br />It is with his involvement that the story redeems itself. The character of Billy Parham stands as one of my all-time favorites. Desirous of new frontiers, haunted by the death of his family, always searching for a new place to call home and forever unable to attain it- he's like the Flying Dutchman on horseback. McCarthy uses him to great effect within these pages, too, as both a vengeful spirit and a barometer for measuring the changing standards of an age as the Southwest moves from the freedom of the open range to the ignorant, militia-enforced, border fence-building, we-don't-hablo-no-espanol standards of today.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10140268930786725553" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3554291420" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3554291420" />
    <a title="Joy D" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/50297885-joy-d"><img alt="Joy D" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1450639623p2/50297885.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3554291420?book_show_action=true">Nov 03, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Joy D" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Joy D" href="/user/show/50297885-joy-d">Joy D</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/50297885-joy-d?shelf=american-west">american-west</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/50297885-joy-d?shelf=north-america">north-america</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/50297885-joy-d?shelf=literary-fiction">literary-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/50297885-joy-d?shelf=relationships">relationships</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/50297885-joy-d?shelf=friendship">friendship</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/50297885-joy-d?shelf=rural">rural</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/50297885-joy-d?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/50297885-joy-d?shelf=animals">animals</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/50297885-joy-d?shelf=zck">zck</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3554291420" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10486727426914841847">This is the third book in McCarthy’s Border Trilogy. While the first two, <i>All the Pretty Horses </i> and <i>The Crossing,</i> can be read in either order, this one should come last. It brings together the protagonists of the first two novels, John Grady Cole and Billy Parham. <br/><br/>It is set in the early 1950’s in the plains around El Paso, Texas and across the border in Ciudad Juarez, Mexico. The plot revolves around John Grady’s romantic interest in Magdalena, a sixteen-year-old Mexican prostitute. Nineteen-ye</span>
  <span id="freeText10486727426914841847" style="display:none">This is the third book in McCarthy’s Border Trilogy. While the first two, <i>All the Pretty Horses </i> and <i>The Crossing,</i> can be read in either order, this one should come last. It brings together the protagonists of the first two novels, John Grady Cole and Billy Parham. <br /><br />It is set in the early 1950’s in the plains around El Paso, Texas and across the border in Ciudad Juarez, Mexico. The plot revolves around John Grady’s romantic interest in Magdalena, a sixteen-year-old Mexican prostitute. Nineteen-year-old John Grady is devoted to Magdalena to the point of obsession. He exhibits a strong-willed personality and the brashness of youth. Billy and ranch owner Mac serve as his mentors. <br /><br />It is written in McCarthy’s signature style with short, direct dialogue. He realistically portrays the Southwestern desert, and the setting becomes, essentially, another character. I particularly like the indelible connection McCarthy establishes between the land and the people who traverse it. Themes include the inevitability of fate and good vs. evil. I doubt anyone that has read McCarthy would expect anything cheery, and this one is no exception. I am glad I read the trilogy. All three books are solid. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="10486727426914841847" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3143825451" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3143825451" />
    <a title="Roman Clodia" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/56111743-roman-clodia"><img alt="Roman Clodia" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1464021031p2/56111743.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3143825451?book_show_action=true">Jan 16, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Roman Clodia" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Roman Clodia" href="/user/show/56111743-roman-clodia">Roman Clodia</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Unknown Binding 9780330369046" href="/book/show/993768.Cities_of_the_Plain">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3143825451" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10689615023457945278"><i>- A man is always right to pursue the thing he loves. <br/>- No matter even if it kills him?<br/>- I think so. Yes. No matter even that.</i><br/><br/>The final volume in McCarthy's grand trilogy which brings together John Grady Cole from <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/90816.All_the_Pretty_Horses' title='All the Pretty Horses by Cormac McCarthy' rel='nofollow noopener'>All the Pretty Horses</a> and Billy Parham from <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/744388.The_Crossing__The_Border_Trilogy___2_' title='The Crossing (The Border Trilogy, #2) by Cormac McCarthy' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Crossing</a>, now working together on a ranch on the U.S.-Mexico border which is about to be taken over by the military. And the last dying days of an anachronistic cowboy lifestyle is matched by other endings.<br/><br/>Repetition structures these book</span>
  <span id="freeText10689615023457945278" style="display:none"><i>- A man is always right to pursue the thing he loves. <br />- No matter even if it kills him?<br />- I think so. Yes. No matter even that.</i><br /><br />The final volume in McCarthy's grand trilogy which brings together John Grady Cole from <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/90816.All_the_Pretty_Horses" title="All the Pretty Horses by Cormac McCarthy" rel="nofollow noopener">All the Pretty Horses</a> and Billy Parham from <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/744388.The_Crossing__The_Border_Trilogy___2_" title="The Crossing (The Border Trilogy, #2) by Cormac McCarthy" rel="nofollow noopener">The Crossing</a>, now working together on a ranch on the U.S.-Mexico border which is about to be taken over by the military. And the last dying days of an anachronistic cowboy lifestyle is matched by other endings.<br /><br />Repetition structures these books as, yet again, the events of Pretty Horses play out in a different key: another doomed love affair sits at its heart, with border crossings and journeys, and another tense fight that recalls the one in the prison. What is different here is the chorus of cowboys against whom Billy and John Grady stand out: there is camaraderie, if only for a limited time, and humour, a fragile sense of possible stability till a visit to a Mexican brothel throws idealistic John Grady back into a ferocious dream.<br /><br />These books are all about grand romance: women are fatally beautiful yet can never quite be possessed; men are stoical and laconic, their few words belying their depth of feeling; the landscape is raw and magnificent, a monumental backdrop against which the futile and temporary dreams of men play out; and a haunting sense of fatedness and death hovers. <br /><br />McCarthy's prose is spectacular: stark and beautiful, stripped of apostrophes and speech marks, matching the typography to the elemental rawness of the story. And the violence when it comes is brutal and painful, exploding in tense scenes from which we cannot look away. <br /><br />As a trilogy I'd say this is somewhat repetitive and it's worth leaving some space between the individual books - but it's unlike anything else I've read: the brooding landscapes of Hardy combined with the blood-sex themes of DH Lawrence, all given a U.S. makeover of cowboy mythology. And stunning, gorgeous writing from McCarthy who is a supreme prose stylist.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10689615023457945278" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2281105258" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2281105258" />
    <a title="Chadwick" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2518644-chadwick"><img alt="Chadwick" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1473911404p2/2518644.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2281105258?book_show_action=true">Mar 03, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Chadwick" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Chadwick" href="/user/show/2518644-chadwick">Chadwick</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2281105258" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10017734192904576171">This completes the Border Trilogy for me, which I've been slowly savoring for many months, sometimes in print form and sometimes audio. The trilogy is a remarkable achievement, and it has been a memorable reading journey for me.<br/><br/>CITIES OF THE PLAIN may be the weakest of the three books, but perhaps I quibble, and I prefer to think of the trilogy as a whole anyway. For all the harshness and violence in these novels, what will stick with me are the haunting beauty and profound sadness.<br/><br/>I happened to</span>
  <span id="freeText10017734192904576171" style="display:none">This completes the Border Trilogy for me, which I've been slowly savoring for many months, sometimes in print form and sometimes audio. The trilogy is a remarkable achievement, and it has been a memorable reading journey for me.<br /><br />CITIES OF THE PLAIN may be the weakest of the three books, but perhaps I quibble, and I prefer to think of the trilogy as a whole anyway. For all the harshness and violence in these novels, what will stick with me are the haunting beauty and profound sadness.<br /><br />I happened to be reading Robert Macfarlane's fine book THE OLD WAYS at the same time. In it he describes his sculptor friend's affection for a giant boulder as paradoxically "cold and hard and tender." It struck me that that may be an apt phrase to describe the Border Trilogy.<br /><br />This is incredibly affecting work. I won't be forgetting John Grady Cole and the Parham brothers any time soon.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10017734192904576171" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Wayne Barrett" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Wayne Barrett" href="/user/show/21372390-wayne-barrett">Wayne Barrett</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/21372390-wayne-barrett?shelf=historical">historical</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1623521511" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13139453427018743558"><br/>4.5<br/><br/>McCarthy is a master. My only complaint is that he hasn't written more books. <br/><br/>This is the third and final book of the Border trilogy, a gritty, down to earth tale of two friends, John Cole Grady and Billy Parham and their cowboying adventures between the States and the Mexican border. <br/><br/>This one seemed to be more dialogue driven than any of the others, the characters carrying us through the story with their voice, done so well by McCarthy that a lot of backdrop description is not needed. To say</span>
  <span id="freeText13139453427018743558" style="display:none"><br />4.5<br /><br />McCarthy is a master. My only complaint is that he hasn't written more books. <br /><br />This is the third and final book of the Border trilogy, a gritty, down to earth tale of two friends, John Cole Grady and Billy Parham and their cowboying adventures between the States and the Mexican border. <br /><br />This one seemed to be more dialogue driven than any of the others, the characters carrying us through the story with their voice, done so well by McCarthy that a lot of backdrop description is not needed. To say much more about the impact of this book on my emotions would be a spoiler, so let's just say, considering I am an avid Cormack fan I should have been prepared, but... well, that's why I say he is a master.</span>

<div id="review_1424323655" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1424323655" />
    <a title="Daniel Chaikin" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/18695911-daniel-chaikin"><img alt="Daniel Chaikin" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1534027159p2/18695911.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1424323655?book_show_action=true">Oct 24, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Daniel Chaikin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Daniel Chaikin" href="/user/show/18695911-daniel-chaikin">Daniel Chaikin</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1424323655" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3046496295197386607">65. <b><a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/40470.Cities_of_the_Plain__The_Border_Trilogy___3_' title='Cities of the Plain (The Border Trilogy, #3) by Cormac McCarthy' rel='nofollow noopener'>Cities of the Plain</a> by <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/4178.Cormac_McCarthy' title='Cormac McCarthy' rel='nofollow noopener'>Cormac McCarthy</a></b> (1998, 292 pages within an <i>Everyman's Library</i> Hardcover edition of <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6163.The_Border_Trilogy_All_the_Pretty_Horses__The_Crossing__Cities_of_the_Plain' title='The Border Trilogy All the Pretty Horses, The Crossing, Cities of the Plain by Cormac McCarthy' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Border Trilogy: All the Pretty Horses, The Crossing, Cities of the Plain</a>, read Oct 4 - 18)<br/>Rating: 4.5 stars<br/><br/>John Grady Cole and Billy Parham finally meet up as ranch hands on an old New Mexico ranch run a old man, Johnson. Johnson is going slowly senile. He walks in his sleep, looks defeated and lost after the somewhat recent death of his daughter. His ranch is run by his son-in-law, </span>
  <span id="freeText3046496295197386607" style="display:none">65. <b><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/40470.Cities_of_the_Plain__The_Border_Trilogy___3_" title="Cities of the Plain (The Border Trilogy, #3) by Cormac McCarthy" rel="nofollow noopener">Cities of the Plain</a> by <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/4178.Cormac_McCarthy" title="Cormac McCarthy" rel="nofollow noopener">Cormac McCarthy</a></b> (1998, 292 pages within an <i>Everyman's Library</i> Hardcover edition of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6163.The_Border_Trilogy_All_the_Pretty_Horses__The_Crossing__Cities_of_the_Plain" title="The Border Trilogy All the Pretty Horses, The Crossing, Cities of the Plain by Cormac McCarthy" rel="nofollow noopener">The Border Trilogy: All the Pretty Horses, The Crossing, Cities of the Plain</a>, read Oct 4 - 18)<br />Rating: 4.5 stars<br /><br />John Grady Cole and Billy Parham finally meet up as ranch hands on an old New Mexico ranch run a old man, Johnson. Johnson is going slowly senile. He walks in his sleep, looks defeated and lost after the somewhat recent death of his daughter. His ranch is run by his son-in-law, Mac, but is about to taken by the US military. Let's call it 1952. Juarez, Mexico is the city of the plain. It's twin El Paso gives the title its plural, but doesn't get touched on all that deeply.<br /><br />I found it interesting to learn <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/40470.Cities_of_the_Plain__The_Border_Trilogy___3_" title="Cities of the Plain (The Border Trilogy, #3) by Cormac McCarthy" rel="nofollow noopener">Cities of the Plain</a> was the first book of the Trilogy written. McCarthy felt he needed to flesh out the back story for the book to work. So, John Grady and Billy each got their own book. But McCarthy's writing evolved and the first two books are probably better than this one, making <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/40470.Cities_of_the_Plain__The_Border_Trilogy___3_" title="Cities of the Plain (The Border Trilogy, #3) by Cormac McCarthy" rel="nofollow noopener">Cities of the Plain</a> somewhat anticlimactic. Also, I don't think the extra talkative Billy here can have come from the reticent, uncommunicative Billy in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/365990.The_Crossing__The_Border_Trilogy___2_" title="The Crossing (The Border Trilogy, #2) by Cormac McCarthy" rel="nofollow noopener">The Crossing</a>. <br /><br />But still I really got into this book, loved reading it, was struck by the end, and felt I had some insight into what drove McCarthy onto this trilogy. It's worth noting The Border Trilogy marks a major change in McCarthy's style. Gone are the hypnotic language-bending lexicon of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/394469.Suttree" title="Suttree by Cormac McCarthy" rel="nofollow noopener">Suttree</a> or <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/394535.Blood_Meridian__or_the_Evening_Redness_in_the_West" title="Blood Meridian, or the Evening Redness in the West by Cormac McCarthy" rel="nofollow noopener">Blood Meridian</a>. The language in the trilogy is toned down and very direct, where English. (There is a great deal of dialogue in Spanish.)<br /><br /><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[Some of my thoughts on this book revolve around a major spoiler. Don't take this warning lightly. Quit here if you might read the book. The end gives the whole series a Romeo and Juliet feel. I also had the sense McCarthy was killing off a part of himself. John Grady represents a purity youth, and the beauty of a simplistic, focused, logic. This is a whole side of all of McCarthy's writing...the plain, heartless yet pure logic to life and nature. In JG it's also given a heart. And killing him off is maybe the author shedding or losing this idealism. The world is a complicated place. And McCarthy lives on by continuing to search it for answers. In that sense, he evolves from JG into a lesser but wiser, but still hopeless Billy.  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />One affect of the end of the this story is the emotional thrumming it can leave in the reader. I was kind of stunned by the direction this went, and left in an agitated state for the books final, lengthy section; and in that state that section became one of the most interesting of the trilogy. An elder, homeless Billy meets another homeless man from Mexico and gets a tale. The man tells of a dream of man within his own dream. It's chopped to death, this dream, by the dialogue of Billy and the unnamed (?) Mexican. Billy raking him with blunt questions about the point and about how he knows what he has seen. The Mexican responds formally, elegantly, with patience, about the disconnect between reality and the dream, and the hopeless logic of trying to gather how one can dream of another dreaming and still give the story a logic that has some consistency with reality. <blockquote>In any case it is difficult to stand outside of one’s desires and see things of their own volition.<br /><br />I think you just see whatever’s in front of you.<br /><br />Yes. I don’t think that. </blockquote>...<br />about the dreaming traveler inside the dream<br />... <blockquote>How do you know he was asleep?<br /><br />I could see him sleeping.<br /><br />Did he dream?<br /><br />The man sat looking at his shoes. He uncrossed his legs and recrossed them the other way. Well, he said. I’m not sure how to answer you. Certain events occurred. Some things about them remain unclear. It is difficult to know, for instance, when it was that these events took place.<br /><br />Why?<br /><br />The dream I had was on a certain night. And in the dream the traveler appeared. What night was this? In the life of the traveler when was it that he came to spend the night in that rocky posada? He slept and events took place which I will tell you of, but when was this? You can see the problem. Let us say that the events which took place were a dream of this man whose own reality remains conjectural. How assess the world of that conjectural mind? And what with him is sleep and what is waking? How comes he to own a world of night at all? Things need a ground to stand upon. As every soul requires a body. A dream within a dream makes other claims than what a man might suppose. <br /><br />A dream inside a dream might not be a dream<br /><br />You have to consider the possibility.</blockquote>It's a dialogue about story telling, but one between two dramatically different characters that seem to define the whole trilogy. I think McCarthy's fascination with Mexico comes in a bit more clearly here. He loves the contrast between the blunt American cowboys and the stylized Mexican avoidance of such. Both are very much interested in the same thing, vaguely McCarthy's search of understanding. <blockquote>All knowledge is a borrowing and every fact a debt. For each event is revealed to us only at the surrender of every alternate course.</blockquote>But the path they take and the mindset toward there are quite different. And in their meeting something is revealed about stories and their telling. <br /><br />This last section is just to close my review:<blockquote>Although I should point out that you are the one with the questions.<br /><br />No you shouldnt.<br /><br />Yes. Of course.<br /><br />Just get on with it.<br /><br />Yes.<br /><br />Mum's the word here.<br /><br />Cómo?<br /><br />Nothing. I'll shut up askin questions, that's all.<br /><br />They were good questions.<br /><br />You aint goin to tell the story, are you?</blockquote></span>
  <a data-text-id="3046496295197386607" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3091596106" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3091596106" />
    <a title="Bob Brinkmeyer" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/34401683-bob-brinkmeyer"><img alt="Bob Brinkmeyer" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1580600423p2/34401683.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3091596106?book_show_action=true">Dec 19, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Bob Brinkmeyer" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Bob Brinkmeyer" href="/user/show/34401683-bob-brinkmeyer">Bob Brinkmeyer</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3091596106" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18164012073180783451">Cities of the Plain continues the stories of John Grady Cole, the protagonist of All the Pretty Horses, and Billy Parham, the hero of The Crossing. When we had last seen both characters, each was adrift and aimless, having returned from harrowing trips into Mexico. Each’s future was unclear. John Grady was riding off into the desert, the shadows from his and the two horses he’s traveling with “paled into the darkening land, the world to come.” Billy was weeping for all he had lost, having awoken</span>
  <span id="freeText18164012073180783451" style="display:none">Cities of the Plain continues the stories of John Grady Cole, the protagonist of All the Pretty Horses, and Billy Parham, the hero of The Crossing. When we had last seen both characters, each was adrift and aimless, having returned from harrowing trips into Mexico. Each’s future was unclear. John Grady was riding off into the desert, the shadows from his and the two horses he’s traveling with “paled into the darkening land, the world to come.” Billy was weeping for all he had lost, having awoken in the desert on the morning of the Trinity nuclear bomb test; it was a new morning for the world with the advent of nuclear weapons, but for Billy it was merely the start of another day, a cold wind “shearing off the western slopes of the continent” and the sun rising, “once again, for all without distinction.” Bleak times, in other words, for both young men.<br /><br />Now several years later, John Grady and Billy in Cities have both ended up working on a ranch in New Mexico. At first glance, their futures seem secure: they have a place in a supportive ranch community and their work—as ranch hands—is what they are good at and what they have always wanted. But, as quickly becomes clear, they actually have no security, as a nearby military base, expanding its reach, is on the verge of annexing the ranch. “We’ll all be goin somewhere when the army takes this spread over,” John Grady comments. And Billy, picking up on a theme he had talked about during his earlier experiences—the military’s remaking of the West—observes: “Anyway this country aint the same. Nor anything in it. The war changed everthing. I dont think people even know it yet.”<br /><br />The romance of Mexico that had previously fired the imaginations of John Grady and Billy is also no more. Once beckoning to them as a land of mystery and adventure—a version of the legendary West, now that the West was fenced and transformed—Mexico in Cities is now merely a border town where the two men can go for cheap liquor and prostitutes. It’s significant that Cities opens in a Mexican brothel, with John Grady, Billy, and a friend dickering over the choice of women—that’s what adventure in Mexico has become for them.<br /><br />As in the trilogy’s previous novels, John Grady and Billy possess a fundamental goodness that doesn’t always serve them well in a world that doesn’t operate by goodness. John Grady’s attraction to and falling in love with a young Mexican prostitute, and his dream of bringing her to the US to start a new life with her, begin a cascade of events leading to catastrophe. John Grady means well, but that amounts to little in the end. A conversation between John Grady and Billy captures the novel’s prevailing spirit, John Grady here speaking first about following one’s dreams and ideals:<br /><br />You just try and use your best judgment and that’s about it.<br /><br />Yeah. Well. The world don’t know nothin about your judgment.<br /><br />I know it. It’s worse than that. It don’t care.<br /><br />At the end of the novel, Billy, an old man, is once again adrift in New Mexico. A kind family, however, takes him in, giving him a place and a purpose (to serve witness to what he has learned and to tutor the family’s children in the ways of horses). It’s a surprisingly quiet and domestic conclusion to a trilogy steeped in violence amidst a world frequently described as a “darkening land.” But, as I’ve stated elsewhere, small acts of kindness, particularly to those in need, mask a deep-seated heroism that McCarthy celebrates throughout his fiction. <br /><br />While Cities may lack some of the complexity of the previous novels in the trilogy, it’s still a strong, powerful work. And McCarthy’s prose, as always, is stark and beautiful—or, better put, wondrous.</span>
  <a data-text-id="18164012073180783451" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_60781338" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/60781338" />
    <a title="Amanda" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/31019-amanda"><img alt="Amanda" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/f_50x66-6a03a5c12233c941481992b82eea8d23.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/60781338?book_show_action=true">Jun 23, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Amanda" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Amanda" href="/user/show/31019-amanda">Amanda</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/31019-amanda?shelf=knock-your-socks-off">knock-your-socks-off</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer60781338" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15550476155648691848"><i>When you’re a kid you have these notions about how things are goin to be, Billy said. You get a little older and you pull back some on that. I think you just wind up tryin to minimize the pain. Anyway this country aint the same. Nor anything in it. The war changed everthing. I dont think people even know it yet.</i><br/><br/>The final chapter in McCarthy's Border Trilogy, <i>Cities of the Plain</i> brings together our two sumbitches, John Grady Cole and Billy Parham, cowboying on a ranch in border Texas in the 1950s</span>
  <span id="freeText15550476155648691848" style="display:none"><i>When you’re a kid you have these notions about how things are goin to be, Billy said. You get a little older and you pull back some on that. I think you just wind up tryin to minimize the pain. Anyway this country aint the same. Nor anything in it. The war changed everthing. I dont think people even know it yet.</i><br /><br />The final chapter in McCarthy's Border Trilogy, <i>Cities of the Plain</i> brings together our two sumbitches, John Grady Cole and Billy Parham, cowboying on a ranch in border Texas in the 1950s. In the background, the country teeters on the postwar precipice; and John Grady falls into another star-crossed love. The hallmarks of <i>Horses</i> and <i>Crossing</i> — the lawlessness of the borderlands, the aching beauty and elegant violence, whores and horses, wise Mexican soothsayers, knifefights and blood pooling in a cowboy boot, otra vez, men who don't fit in the world they find themselves in — reach a crescendo here. There's that tragic moment when you think maybe it's going to be okay, maybe these men will find peace, but c'mon, the title is <i>Cities of the Plain</i>, how can we expect anything less than vengeance and destruction?<br /><br />I hated to even start because I didn't want it to end. Then the shit hit the fan — OH MY GOD though, one of the most powerful, heart-rending, masterfully written scenes I've read, I'm trying not to say too much but oh lord — and it was almost too much to take in public (on the subway). I felt a painful sense of loss when I closed the book. Part of it might be because of the intensity of reading three books back to back, but I didn't want the experience to end. I'll never be able to read this for the first time again; while I'll probably read all three again — I am planning a lifelong commitment to McCarthy, he's won a spot in my personal canon — I'll need a while to mourn this first loss.<br /><br />More than a Western, more than a romance, more than gothic, more than violent the book has these things, if that's what you want, but more than that it transcends. It captures something essential about the world through these characters, in this place, in this time. The five stars is more for the trilogy as a whole, I think, but also for <i>Cities of the Plain</i> alone, which consumed my brain and my heart and my guts while I was reading it, reached in and twisted that strange place inside and, in at least some small way, I'm not going to be quite the same from here on out. That's a damn good book.<br /><br />All the Pretty Horses: <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/60779860">http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/...</a><br />The Crossing: <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/60781322">http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/...</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="15550476155648691848" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/166647201?book_show_action=true">May 08, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jeremy" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jeremy" href="/user/show/869064-jeremy">Jeremy</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/869064-jeremy?shelf=american-fiction">american-fiction</a>,

          </div>
