        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer166647201" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12181821328260887443">I was really surprised at just how talkative this book is. It's got more dialogue than anything else Mcarthy's written. Which makes sense because this book evokes not just single lonely lives, but an entire, lonely lifestyle. Cities of the Plain partakes easily of the typical tropes of American westerns, doomed love, the sense of loss for an increasingly marginalized and antiquated cowboy culture at odds with a modernizing west, what have you. But it's much more than just a thin genre piece beca</span>
  <span id="freeText12181821328260887443" style="display:none">I was really surprised at just how talkative this book is. It's got more dialogue than anything else Mcarthy's written. Which makes sense because this book evokes not just single lonely lives, but an entire, lonely lifestyle. Cities of the Plain partakes easily of the typical tropes of American westerns, doomed love, the sense of loss for an increasingly marginalized and antiquated cowboy culture at odds with a modernizing west, what have you. But it's much more than just a thin genre piece because of the two books that come before it and the harsh, painful lives that John and Billy have led up to this point. There aren't as many big blocks of prose here because we already know their hard lives so well from 'the crossing' and 'all the pretty horses'. And by the way, if you haven't read both of those, DO NOT read Cities of the Plain, because there is an entire layer of unspoken experiences and pains that won't make much sense, and which probably won't seem all that interesting without knowing what happened before this. Though I don't think it ranks among his best, this is still a good ending to the trilogy. Heck, I was sad when it ended but that probably has as much to do with the fact that it's the last book of his I haven't read as it did with the ending itself. Billy Parham is probably the best non-evil non-degenerate character he's written. And Eduardo (while not his best villain) is a refreshingly down to earth slime-ball pimp. I hope Mccarthy still has a few books in him.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12181821328260887443" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1904395625" />
    <a title="James" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/41609617-james"><img alt="James" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1539156508p2/41609617.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1904395625?book_show_action=true">Apr 03, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="James" class="user" itemprop="url" name="James" href="/user/show/41609617-james">James</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780330544559" href="/book/show/13118632-cities-of-the-plain">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1904395625" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7766848390996065532">'Cities of the Plain' is Cormac McCarthy's third and concluding part to his acclaimed and revered Border Trilogy.<br/><br/>On the face of it and for the most part, 'Cities' has a more straightforward narrative and dramatic tone than both its Border predecessors ('All the Pretty Horses' and 'The Crossing') and 'Cities' does however have the unenviable task of pulling together and concluding the brilliant Border Trilogy.<br/><br/>That said, 'Cities' does however have its own undoubted power and emotional impact. The </span>
  <span id="freeText7766848390996065532" style="display:none">'Cities of the Plain' is Cormac McCarthy's third and concluding part to his acclaimed and revered Border Trilogy.<br /><br />On the face of it and for the most part, 'Cities' has a more straightforward narrative and dramatic tone than both its Border predecessors ('All the Pretty Horses' and 'The Crossing') and 'Cities' does however have the unenviable task of pulling together and concluding the brilliant Border Trilogy.<br /><br />That said, 'Cities' does however have its own undoubted power and emotional impact. The story of its two main protagonists and a central love affair is a compulsive one. Whilst 'Cities' does function as a standalone novel, to be fully appreciated it really does need to be read as part of the Border trilogy.<br /><br />'Cities of the Plain' is all about the journey that is life, friendship, love and death and much that is in-between - it's about finding your own place, your own journey in life and the beauty and brutality of existence. <br /><br />Disarmingly pedestrian and poetic by turns - 'Cities' provides a satisfying and fulfilling conclusion to McCarthy's fantastic Border Trilogy.<br /> <br /><br /> </span>
  <a data-text-id="7766848390996065532" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_154237825" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

<span id="freeTextContainer9339983253107531786">A beautiful ending to the Border trilogy. <i>The Cities of the Plain</i> is more of a dialogue-driven novel than the previous two (<i>All the Pretty Horses</i>, <i>the Crossing</i>). It is still a novel firmly wrapped in beautiful landscapes, sad existentialism, and a world that is often dry, cold and unforgiving. It is a tale of wild and dying giants, fading men, and setting suns. <br/><br/>I read McCarthy and think: what the most I would trade to take possession of the least of his novels? Would I give an ear for his weakes</span>
  <span id="freeText9339983253107531786" style="display:none">A beautiful ending to the Border trilogy. <i>The Cities of the Plain</i> is more of a dialogue-driven novel than the previous two (<i>All the Pretty Horses</i>, <i>the Crossing</i>). It is still a novel firmly wrapped in beautiful landscapes, sad existentialism, and a world that is often dry, cold and unforgiving. It is a tale of wild and dying giants, fading men, and setting suns. <br /><br />I read McCarthy and think: what the most I would trade to take possession of the least of his novels? Would I give an ear for his weakest writing? An eye? Which is his weakest novel? I am not certain I have found it yet. </span>

  <span id="freeText3879775811000996063" style="display:none"> I may be rating this book a little higher than necessary, but is very good, and especially as the last of his Trilogy Series. I read all three back to back, and normally I could be "cowboyed" out. But only because Cormac is so damn good, I want more horses, more open range, more lonely destructive adventure, Yes I want More.<br /> Cormac is a poet that writes prose and the results are hearty and filling. When your flesh is filleted in a knife fight, the blood fills up your boots. Details. I give you an example "far to the south he could see the thin white adderstongues of lightning licking silently along the rim of the sky in the darkness over Mexico." Adderstongues? Really, but can you better describe the abrupt darting movement of the lightning? No. But Cormac can. Buy his books, give him your money: he is an American Master.</span>

  <div class="left bodycol">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Dan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Dan" href="/user/show/180438-dan">Dan</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer45201752" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14945320253826907200">This book is somewhere between three and four stars. It earns this rating because it is not nearly as good as "All the Pretty Horses," but it's still a great book.<br/><br/>The one major disappointment with this story was its lack of powerful, beautiful, and riveting poetic form. In ATPH and Crossing, the poetry that goes into describing the scenes, people and emotions is just heartbreakingly good. In this story, however, it is noticeably absent until near the end of the book.<br/><br/>The positive: he meant to wri</span>
  <span id="freeText14945320253826907200" style="display:none">This book is somewhere between three and four stars. It earns this rating because it is not nearly as good as "All the Pretty Horses," but it's still a great book.<br /><br />The one major disappointment with this story was its lack of powerful, beautiful, and riveting poetic form. In ATPH and Crossing, the poetry that goes into describing the scenes, people and emotions is just heartbreakingly good. In this story, however, it is noticeably absent until near the end of the book.<br /><br />The positive: he meant to write it that way. The poetry isn't there because John and Billy have grown up a bit. They have settled down from their days of riding the wilderness. However, it's still in them, living down deep, and that's when we see it near the end of the book.<br /><br />Some might say that this story did not wrap up tight enough, but I think this trilogy is a story with no end. That's what McCarthy wanted it to be, at least that's what I gather from the way he writes. There have been human beings like John and Billy since the beginning of time, and there will always be humans like them from here on out.<br /><br />Reading these books gives you a brief glimpse at the darkest and brightest qualities of humankind, and that certainly is a story worth reading.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14945320253826907200" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_47263654" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/47263654" />
    <a title="Brian" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/36120-brian"><img alt="Brian" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1546035836p2/36120.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/47263654?book_show_action=true">Feb 23, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Brian" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Brian" href="/user/show/36120-brian">Brian</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer47263654" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2675045116440173895">They drove on. Rounding a curve with a steep bank to the right of the road there was a sudden white flare and a solid whump of a sound. The truck veered, the tires squealing. When they got stopped they were halfway off the road into the bar ditch.<br/><br/>What in the hell, said Troy. What in the hell.<br/><br/>A large owl lay cruciform across the driver's windshield of the truck. The laminate of the glass was belled in softly to hold him and his wings were spread wide and he lay in the concentric rings and rays of</span>
  <span id="freeText2675045116440173895" style="display:none">They drove on. Rounding a curve with a steep bank to the right of the road there was a sudden white flare and a solid whump of a sound. The truck veered, the tires squealing. When they got stopped they were halfway off the road into the bar ditch.<br /><br />What in the hell, said Troy. What in the hell.<br /><br />A large owl lay cruciform across the driver's windshield of the truck. The laminate of the glass was belled in softly to hold him and his wings were spread wide and he lay in the concentric rings and rays of the wrecked glass like an enormous moth in a web.<br /><br />Billy shut off the engine. They sat looking at it. One of its feet shuddered and drew up into a claw and slowly relaxed again and it moved its head slightly as if to better see them and then it died.<br /><br />* * *<br /><br />When you're a kid you have these notions about how things are goin to be, Billy said. You get a little older and you pull back some on that. I think you wind up just trying to minimize the pain.<br /><br />* * *<br /><br />His wife would be dead three years in February. Socorro's Candlemas Day. Candelaria. Something to do with the Virgin. As what didnt. In Mexico there is no God. Just her.<br /><br />* * *<br /><br />I know it. There's hard lessons in this world.<br /><br />What's the hardest?<br /><br />I dont know. Maybe it's just that when things are gone they're gone. They aint comin back.<br /><br />Yessir.<br /><br />* * *<br /><br />Maybe. Anyway, some men get what they want.<br /><br />No man. Or perhaps only briefly so as to lose it. Or perhaps only to prove to the dreamer that the world of his longing made real is no longer that world at all.<br /><br />* * *<br /><br />I only know that every act that has no heart will be found out in the end. Every gesture.<br /><br />* * *<br /><br />Two owls crouching in the dust of the road turned their pale and heartshaped faces in the trucklights and blinked and rose on their white wings as silent as two souls ascending and vanished in the darkness overhead.<br /><br />* * *<br /><br />You cant tell anybody anything, bud. Hell, it's really just a way of tellin yourself. And you cant even do that. You just try and use your best judgment and that's about it.<br /><br />* * *<br /><br />This is what has brought you here and what will always bring you here. You cannot bear that the world be ordinary. That it contain nothing save what stands before one. But the Mexican world is a world of adornment only and underneath it is very plain indeed. While your world -- he passed the blade back and forth like a shuttle through a loom -- your world totters upon an unspoken labyrinth of questions. And we will devour you, my friend. You and all your pale empire.<br /><br />* * *<br /><br />Hush now. They aint nothin over.<br /><br />* * *<br /><br />The immappable world of our journey. A pass in the mountains. A bloodstained stone. The marks of steel upon it. Names carved in the corrosible lime among stone fishes and ancient shells. Things dimmed and dimming. The dry sea floor. The tools of migrant hunters. The dreams encased upon the blades of them. The peregrine bones of a prophet. The silence. The gradual extinction of rain. The coming of night.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2675045116440173895" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_46692599" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/46692599" />
    <a title="Briynne" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/94054-briynne"><img alt="Briynne" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1225157665p2/94054.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/46692599?book_show_action=true">Feb 17, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Briynne" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Briynne" href="/user/show/94054-briynne">Briynne</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer46692599" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7453292486808215914">It’s probably not a secret at this point that I’m a McCarthy groupie. These books are just so good. This third of the loosely-defined Border Trilogy novels bring together John Grady Cole of <i>All the Pretty Horses</i> and Billy Parham of <i>The Crossing</i>, who are now working on the same ranch in West Texas. Neither of the men’s histories are mentioned in any great detail in this book, but I feel like it would be a horrible shame to read this book without understanding the back-story from the previous two </span>
  <span id="freeText7453292486808215914" style="display:none">It’s probably not a secret at this point that I’m a McCarthy groupie. These books are just so good. This third of the loosely-defined Border Trilogy novels bring together John Grady Cole of <i>All the Pretty Horses</i> and Billy Parham of <i>The Crossing</i>, who are now working on the same ranch in West Texas. Neither of the men’s histories are mentioned in any great detail in this book, but I feel like it would be a horrible shame to read this book without understanding the back-story from the previous two novels. It’s impossible for me to separate Billy from the horror of his final trip to Mexico, or John Grady from his propensity to fall in love with unsuitable women no matter the cost. <br /><br />They are wonderfully true to their established character, but older and changed. Billy is resilient as ever, but colder and harder than he was as a teenager. He mentions his dead little brother once or twice, but he doesn’t wear any of the tragedies on his sleeve. He is more cautious as well, but his heart wins out as it always did. <br /><br />John Grady is still very young in this book – fewer years have passed in his story than in Billy’s – and he is still stupidly romantic but wonderfully graceful. This time, instead of falling for the poor little rich girl, he sets his heart on a 15-year-old Mexican prostitute. He pursues the match with the same quiet, single-minded determinism that he uses in gentling unbroken horses, and even though I wanted him to just quit, he wouldn’t have been himself if he had. Billy sees it all for the insanity that it is, but eventually ends up helping John Grady in his doomed and dangerous plans. <br /><br />There isn’t a happy ending. I knew there couldn’t be one, but it broke my heart all the same. John Grady and the girl knew what was coming for them, but Billy is the one who really suffers. I wished that the author had left out the epilogue in many ways. I didn’t really want to know what happened after that day – I would have preferred to think it ended differently than it did. I really did want a hug after this one, but James was unimpressed by the notion of emotional trauma via literature and advised me to walk it off. <br /><br />And beyond the plot itself, there is this dark and mournful elegy taking place in the background. The war, among other things, is quickly destroying the life these men lead; they know that there will very soon not be a place for them in the world, and it's hard to imagine what that actually feels like. The bleakness of it matches McCarthy's stark style, which was broken up nicely the the occasionally hilarious things his characters would say, in perfect deadpan. <br /><br />I personally think the Border Trilogy should be near the top of everyone's To-Read list. And I don't mean the "maybe someday/I ought to but don't want to/I'm putting this here out of guilt" list. I mean the real one. It's really good, so think about it.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7453292486808215914" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1542318817" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1542318817" />
    <a title="Josh" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/10773168-josh"><img alt="Josh" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1341723484p2/10773168.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1542318817?book_show_action=true">Feb 08, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Josh" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Josh" href="/user/show/10773168-josh">Josh</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780679423904" href="/book/show/239665.Cities_of_the_Plain">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1542318817" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4961457100052455162">Very, very, very subtle McCarthy. Unlike some trilogies, this ends it all up almost perfectly. It is not a read alone (in my opinion)......to do so without first broaching <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/469571.All_the_Pretty_Horses__The_Border_Trilogy___1_' title='All the Pretty Horses (The Border Trilogy, #1) by Cormac McCarthy' rel='nofollow noopener'>All the Pretty Horses</a> and <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/365990.The_Crossing__The_Border_Trilogy___2_' title='The Crossing (The Border Trilogy, #2) by Cormac McCarthy' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Crossing</a> would to be to rob yourself of the crescendo moments that feed this dialogue. It needs that momentum to lift properly.<br/><br/>In this work, John Grady Cole and Billy Parham collectively join up as protagonists in a fitting end to the trilogy- making the points of societies' shifts (plural intende</span>
  <span id="freeText4961457100052455162" style="display:none">Very, very, very subtle McCarthy. Unlike some trilogies, this ends it all up almost perfectly. It is not a read alone (in my opinion)......to do so without first broaching <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/469571.All_the_Pretty_Horses__The_Border_Trilogy___1_" title="All the Pretty Horses (The Border Trilogy, #1) by Cormac McCarthy" rel="nofollow noopener">All the Pretty Horses</a> and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/365990.The_Crossing__The_Border_Trilogy___2_" title="The Crossing (The Border Trilogy, #2) by Cormac McCarthy" rel="nofollow noopener">The Crossing</a> would to be to rob yourself of the crescendo moments that feed this dialogue. It needs that momentum to lift properly.<br /><br />In this work, John Grady Cole and Billy Parham collectively join up as protagonists in a fitting end to the trilogy- making the points of societies' shifts (plural intended), man's place in the web of events we call life, and the futility to shape it in anyway other than perceived impacts. Beloved Mexico and all her charms still wreaks havoc and teaches the characters (and reader) all about the cores in all of us.<br /><br />Cormac ambles along until roughly page 190 or so, when two wise men (Mr. Johnson and the maestro) begin urging the themes of the entire trilogy (or for that matter most of Cormac's writing). I will hold off much commentary about those themes. I will also comment that Cormac's genius in this case is that all three books........as unrelated to the present day as they each seem were in my opinion all written as a set up to the Epilouge of this book. In the same way as <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/394469.Suttree" title="Suttree by Cormac McCarthy" rel="nofollow noopener">Suttree</a> was written to set up the final lines of the book. He begs us to stay in touch long enough to draw us up on his stage and then preach like Hell to us........and I for one read all three volumes just waiting for the invitation. In this case, all three books, published over a five year span all boil down to a 30 page epilogue.......you foxy devil you. Heck, for that matter, it may have all been written just to write the 28 word dedication, but I'm still studying on that.<br /><br />My google account is reeling from all the fun words........I'm likely now on some kind of watch list, but that probably happened five McCarthy books ago. My favorite passage this time around:<br /><br />"Bear with me, the man said. This story like all stories has its beginnings in a question. And those stories which speak to us with the greatest resonance have a way of turning upon the teller and erasing him and his motives from all memory. So the question of who is telling the story is a very consiguiente. <br /><br />Every story is not about some question.<br /><br />Yes it is. Where all is known no narrative is possible."<br /><br />Well, that may be true Mr. McCarthy........and it's sure a fun ride trying to answer your questions. Where your quote doesn't hit that mark is that your stories speak to us; yet, its impossible to erase you or your motives.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4961457100052455162" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('TUh3ME8Z2dQMph1nEDBIkRlGpjCOrpI9NzodFWSM/zH+jt7eftytChmv+EpeqZAcznB/TaXmtXPLFgBBfEW0wA==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('kKiAvQSQQuVrXHCYA3V9wmdah5ZK2yefwdgIw1o83+EjbilTNVU2O35VlbVN7KVPsGxe62GTANE99BWXQvWUEA==')})
  }

Written in what DeLillo calls &quot;super-omniscience&quot; the sentences sweep from young Cotter Martin as he jumps the gate to the press box, soars over the radio waves, runs out to the diamond, slides in on a fast ball, pops into the stands where J. Edgar Hoover is sitting with a drunken Jackie Gleason and a splenetic Frank Sinatra, and learns of the Soviet Union\'s second detonation of a nuclear bomb. It\'s an absolutely thrilling literary moment. When Bobby Thomson hits Branca\'s pitch into the outstretched hand of Cotter—the &quot;shot heard around the world&quot;—and Jackie Gleason pukes on Sinatra\'s shoes, the events of the next few decades are set in motion, all threaded together by the baseball as it passes from hand to hand.\n\n&quot;It\'s all falling indelibly into the past,&quot; writes DeLillo, a past that he carefully recalls and reconstructs with acute grace. Jump from Giants Stadium to the Nevada desert in 1992, where Nick Shay, who now owns the baseball, reunites with the artist Kara Sax. They had been brief and unlikely lovers 40 years before, and it is largely through the events, spinoffs, and coincidental encounters of their pasts that DeLillo filters the Cold War experience. He believes that &quot;global events may alter how we live in the smallest ways,&quot; and as the book steps back in time to 1951, over the following 800-odd pages, we see just how those events alter lives. This reverse narrative allows the author to strip away the detritus of history and pop culture until we get to the story\'s pure elements: the bomb, the baseball, and the Bronx. In an epilogue as breathless and stunning as the prologue, DeLillo fast-forwards to a near future in which ruthless capitalism, the Internet, and a new, hushed faith have replaced the Cold War\'s blend of dread and euphoria.\n\nThrough fragments and interlaced stories—including those of highway killers, artists, celebrities, conspiracists, gangsters, nuns, and sundry others—DeLillo creates a fragile web of connected experience, a communal Zeitgeist that encompasses the messy whole of five decades of American life, wonderfully distilled.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"13644895246346524421\" Pronghorn antelope, gray wolves, bison, coyotes, wild horses, and grizzly bears: less than two hundred years ago these creatures existed in such abundance that John James Audubon was moved to write, &quot;it is impossible to describe or even conceive the vast multitudes of these animals.&quot;\n\n\n\nIn a work that is at once a lyrical evocation of that lost splendor and a detailed natural history of these charismatic species of the historic Great Plains, veteran naturalist and outdoorsman Dan Flores draws a vivid portrait of each of these animals in their glory--and tells the harrowing story of what happened to them at the hands of market hunters and ranchers and ultimately a federal killing program in the nineteenth and twentieth centuries. The Great Plains with its wildlife intact dazzled Americans and Europeans alike, prompting numerous literary tributes. American Serengeti takes its place alongside these celebratory works, showing us the grazers and predators of the plains against the vast opalescent distances, the blue mountains shimmering on the horizon, the great rippling tracts of yellowed grasslands. Far from the empty &quot;flyover country&quot; of recent times, this landscape is alive with a complex ecology at least 20,000 years old--a continental patrimony whose wonders may not be entirely lost, as recent efforts hold out hope of partial restoration of these historic species.\n\nWritten by an author who has done breakthrough work on the histories of several of these animals--including bison, wild horses, and coyotes--American Serengeti is as rigorous in its research as it is intimate in its sense of wonder--the most deeply informed, closely observed view we have of the Great       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/4178.Cormac_McCarthy">About Cormac McCarthy</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/4178.Cormac_McCarthy"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1593872299p3/4178.jpg);'></div>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/4178.Cormac_McCarthy">Cormac McCarthy
</a></div>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer2413144658038179473">Cormac McCarthy is an American novelist and playwright. He has written ten novels in the Southern Gothic, western, and post-apocalyptic genres and has also written plays and screenplays. He received the Pulitzer Prize in 2007 for <i>The Road</i>, and his 2005 novel <i>No Country for Old Men</i> was adapted as a 2007 film of the same name, which won four Academy Awards, including Best Picture.<br/><br/>His earlier <i>Blood M</i></span>
  <span id="freeText2413144658038179473" style="display:none">Cormac McCarthy is an American novelist and playwright. He has written ten novels in the Southern Gothic, western, and post-apocalyptic genres and has also written plays and screenplays. He received the Pulitzer Prize in 2007 for <i>The Road</i>, and his 2005 novel <i>No Country for Old Men</i> was adapted as a 2007 film of the same name, which won four Academy Awards, including Best Picture.<br /><br />His earlier <i>Blood Meridian</i> (1985) was among <i>Time Magazine's</i> poll of 100 best English-language books published between 1925 and 2005 and he placed joint runner-up for a similar title in a poll taken in 2006 by <i>The New York Times</i> of the best American fiction published in the last 25 years. Literary critic Harold Bloom named him as one of the four major American novelists of his time, along with Thomas Pynchon, Don DeLillo, and Philip Roth. He is frequently compared by modern reviewers to William Faulkner.<br /><br />In 2009, Cormac McCarthy won the PEN/Saul Bellow Award, a lifetime achievement award given by the PEN American Center.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2413144658038179473" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>

First comes a trespassing Indian and the dream of wolves running wild amongst the cattle lately brought onto the plain by settlers - this when all the wisdom of trappers has disappeared along with the trappers themselves. So Billy sets forth at the age of sixteen on an unwitting journey into the souls of boys, animals and men.\n\nHaving trapped a she-wolf he would restore to the mountains of Mexico, he is long gone and returns to find everything he left behind transformed utterly in his absence. Except his kid brother, Boyd, with whom he strikes out yet again to reclaim what is theirs - thus crossing into &quot;that antique gaze from whence there could be no way back forever.&quot;\n\nWhat they find instead, is an extraordinary panoply of fiestas and circuses, dogs, horses and hawks, pilgrims and revolutionaries, grand haciendas and forlorn cantinas, bandits, gypsies and roving tribes, a young girl alone on the road, a mystery in the mountain wilds, and a myth in the making.\n\nAnd in this wider world they fight a war as rageful as the one neither, in the end, will join up for back home. One brother finds his destiny, while the other arrives only at his fate.\n\nAn essential novel by any measure, and the transfixing middle passage of Cormac McCarthy\'s ongoing trilogy, The Crossing is
